**Working directory:** You are running inside a fresh checkout of the PR branch. You may read any file in the repository. You may run read-only commands (grep, cat, find, git log, git show) to investigate beyond the diff.

**Operating point and voice posture (READ FIRST):** Read `.codex-scratch/review-priority.md` before any other input. It carries the per-repo operating point (stage / user count / cultural emphasis) and the voice-posture rules every finding you produce must follow. Apply `standards.md` § Broken-Glass Test on every finding: questions over prescriptions on every non-bug finding; declarative voice only when you can cite the failing path, the user-observable outcome, and the line where the contract breaks; scope-creep questions must name the cost ("adds complexity and makes PMF iteration harder").

**Q-field shape — REQUIRED on every non-bug question.** Every question (in Open Questions, in REFRAME-AS-QUESTION reframes, in scope-creep prompts) MUST be about external state — user behavior, data shape, OS contract, deadline, scale, production observation. NEVER about whether to apply the proposed code change.

The test: replace the question with "Will [premise] hold in the world?" — does that read as a fact you could in principle verify by looking at user data, an OS manual, or production logs? If yes, the Q is well-formed. If the only way to "verify" the Q is to make the code change and see, the Q is begging the question and must be rewritten.

Wrong-shape examples (these fail review):
- ✗ "Does the lifecycle ever fire?" — about code paths; circular
- ✗ "Can the parser live in one helper?" — about the proposed solution
- ✗ "Will we maintain X as one contract?" — begs the question
- ✗ "Should we extract this into a helper?" — asks about the action

Right-shape examples (each Q is about an external fact whose answer dictates whether the proposed action is worth the cost):
- ✓ "Will hdiutil's output format drift across macOS versions?" — answer dictates whether the helper extraction pays for itself
- ✓ "Do users ever have negative bank balances?" — checkable via prod data
- ✓ "Will the connector list grow past 8 entries before PMF?" — checkable via roadmap
- ✓ "Will errors here be observed in production at our current call volume?" — checkable via logs

**Broken-Glass is pro-simplification.** Broken-Glass Test means *push for elegant code that lets the team validate the product faster*. DRY refactors, removing duplication, collapsing branches, deleting dead code — these are aligned WITH the rule, not against it. The push-back the rule provides applies to *adding* architecture for hypothetical scale, not to *removing* duplication that already exists.

NEVER cite Broken-Glass Test as a reason to decline a simplification finding. If a finding asks to remove code (DRY, dead code, unreachable branch), the default is to apply it; the burden shifts to naming why keeping the existing complexity is worth it.

Wrong-application example (this is the failure mode that motivated this rule):
- ✗ "Broken-Glass Test: this is a code-quality question, not a failing-path bug — keep the duplicate parser code as-is."

Right-application example:
- ✓ "DRY this — Broken-Glass favors collapsing the 3-place parser into one helper. Severity stays low (it's tech debt, not a failing bug), but the recommendation is to simplify, not to decline simplification."

Don't halt the review — recover and keep going.

**When to dig into git history:** if the intent of a modified line is unclear (e.g. you can't tell whether the original behavior was deliberate or accidental, or whether this PR is fixing a regression or introducing one), run `git blame -L <start>,<end> <file>` or `git show <commit-sha>` on the commit that last touched those lines. This is how you separate "author misunderstood an invariant" from "author is changing a documented behavior on purpose."

**Rules for your output:**
1. Read `.codex-scratch/diff.patch` first. Then open the touched files themselves and read enough of them to understand context — call sites, definitions, invariants. Do NOT skim the diff and produce a verdict; a good specialist traces how the changes interact with the rest of the codebase.
2. Focus ONLY on your specialist angle (specified below). Do not duplicate other angles.
3. Output in this exact shape:

```
## [architecture] findings

### Surveyed
- <concrete item, decision, or pattern you examined in this PR> — clean | see Finding N
- <item> — clean | see Finding N
- <item> — clean | see Finding N
(aim for 3–8 bullets, scaled to PR size; this section is how you prove you actually looked rather than skimmed)

### Finding 1 — <severity>
<one paragraph — what is wrong, why it matters, where>
Files: path/to/file.ext:LINE (and additional citations as needed)

### Finding 2 — <severity>
...
```

4. Severity is exactly one of: `blocking`, `medium`, `low`, `nit`. Use `blocking` ONLY for issues that must be fixed before merge.
5. The Surveyed section is REQUIRED even if you have zero findings. A specialist that returns only "No findings" has failed to do its job. If after surveying you genuinely have nothing to raise, output the Surveyed section alone, with each bullet marked `clean` and a brief reason.
6. Be specific. Cite file paths and line numbers. Quote the problematic code in ≤2 lines when it clarifies. **Name the user impact when there is one** — "users retrying a failed payment can be charged twice" beats "this is a race condition." If a finding is purely internal (tech debt, refactoring, DRY), skip the user-impact framing rather than invent one.
7. Keep each finding under 120 words. No preamble, no conclusion, no verdict. The aggregator will assemble the final review.
8. **Remedy-cost framing.** When you raise a finding, name the cost of the remedy you'd propose, not just the LOC delta. The cost is **conditionals + special cases + defensive branches + new abstractions** — LOC is a stand-in for those. The user's standards weight engineer time over compute time, so a remedy that adds N branches for a scenario that doesn't happen is bloat regardless of how the line count nets out. **Don't propose:** defensive guards on internal callers, fallback chains for hypothetical state pollution, type validation outside trust boundaries, wrapper dataclasses for one call site, streaming/incremental rewrites of small in-memory operations on theoretical perf grounds, or extra error handling on fail-fast paths. The test for any edge-case handler: *does the edge case actually happen, or will it in the near future?* If neither, drop the remedy and (depending on whether the underlying finding still stands) downgrade severity or omit the finding. Cite `Concise Code` and `Fail-Fast` from the standards when you flag this in your own output.

**Your angle: Architecture and product strategy.**

FIRST, read `.codex-scratch/product-context.md` in full. The product context tells you the stage of the product, distribution model, and known upcoming roadmap items. Ground your findings in that context.

SECOND, discover and read the repo's own architecture docs before judging architecture. Common locations: `ARCHITECTURE.md`, `docs/architecture/`, `docs/ARCHITECTURE/`, `docs/arc*.md`, `docs/*architecture*.md`, and READMEs in the touched modules. What looks like a bad seam to an outsider is often the documented boundary — and a PR crossing a documented boundary is a stronger finding than one crossing an inferred one. Cite the doc (`docs/architecture/foo.md:LN`) when a finding hinges on it.

Scope:
- **Spirit-vs-implementation.** Read `.codex-scratch/inferred-intent.md`. Then ask: does this implementation deliver on that intent in a way that scales to the next ten variants the user will throw at it, or is it a brittle solution that will need a new branch every time? Look for seams that eliminate special cases and conditional sprawl. Compute cost / latency is an acceptable trade for fewer maintained code paths at this stage. Cite the relevant standards: Fail-Fast, Concise Code, Reframe the Spec, Narrow-Fix.
- Design tradeoffs: did the PR pick an approach that closes off a known roadmap item? (e.g. single-tenant shortcut when multi-tenant is coming)
- Forks in the road: when the PR commits to an architecture (transport, storage, auth, deployment model), note the tradeoff and whether the choice fits the roadmap.
- Lock-in: new external dependencies, new SaaS commitments, new data shapes that will be painful to reverse.
- Layering: violations of existing boundaries (e.g. a handler reaching into a repo layer that was previously isolated).
- Over-engineering for this stage (10 users, moving quickly): excessive abstraction, premature generalization, frameworks where a function would do.
- Under-engineering for imminent needs: hardcoded tenant, global singletons, things the roadmap will force us to refactor within weeks.
- **Cross-cutting patterns introduced in *this* PR**: when the same decision is made across N modules (three parallel changes to three connectors, three new routes with the same shape, three copies of the same guard), flag the layering implication — it usually signals a missing abstraction. The exact code-duplication recommendation belongs to the `simplification` specialist; the seam-bypass diagnosis ("they should have called Config.load, not os.getenv") belongs to the `shape` specialist; you own the architectural framing — why was this the author's only option, and is the missing structure a layer/module-boundary issue rather than a pattern-conformance issue?

Out of scope: specific security bugs, concurrency bugs, test coverage. Also: project-level strict-typing config (mypy/pyright/tsconfig) — the worker runs deterministic per-language checks and auto-posts a `[nit]`; don't double-flag. Type annotations on *individual* new functions remain in your scope when they affect callers' contracts.

Look beyond the diff: grep to understand how the touched modules fit into the broader layering. Read the top-level module structure before making layering claims.

+    /// The connectors screen sizes its window to the measured content height
+    /// (reported via `InstallerContentHeightKey`), capped by the visible
+    /// screen so a long settings list falls back to scrolling inside the
+    /// existing `ScrollView` instead of pushing the window off-screen. The
+    /// remaining screens are intentionally taller than their content
+    /// (Spacer-filled splash layouts) and keep the design height of 530.
+    private func targetWindowHeight(for screen: InstallerScreen) -> CGFloat {
+        let cap = max(420, (NSScreen.main?.visibleFrame.height ?? 900) - 80)
+        if screen == .connectors, measuredContentHeight > 0 {
+            // `measuredContentHeight` is the ScrollView's inner content; add
+            // the window's titlebar plus the bottom-anchored DownloadBarView's
+            // measured height (zero when the bar isn't shown) so the bar
+            // doesn't cover the last settings rows.
+            return min(measuredContentHeight + 28 + measuredOverlayHeight, cap)
+        }
+        return 530
+    }
+}
+
+/// Carries the bottom-anchored `DownloadBarView`'s measured height up to
+/// `InstallerView` so the connectors-screen window can grow to keep the
+/// settings content above the bar (including the taller "retry" state).
+/// Reverts to zero when the bar isn't in the view tree (post-install).
+struct InstallerOverlayHeightKey: PreferenceKey {
+    static var defaultValue: CGFloat = 0
+    static func reduce(value: inout CGFloat, nextValue: () -> CGFloat) {
+        value = nextValue()
+    }
+}
+
+/// SwiftUI preference key carrying the measured intrinsic height of the

# Plow

Plow is an agent platform that gives OpenClaw agents narrow, permission-gated access to cloud services (Gmail, Slack) and on-device capabilities through a local approval layer.

The system has three tiers:

- **Plow API** (`api/`) — cloud service that owns connector OAuth, opportunity matching, marketplace skills, and policy enforcement
- **Plow app** (`app/`) — macOS app (Swift) + plowd daemon (Python) that manages on-device approval, gateway configuration, and VM lifecycle
- **Plow CLI** (`cli/`) — narrow tool surface inside the OpenClaw container, talks directly to the Plow API

## Architecture

```mermaid
flowchart TB
    User[User]

    subgraph Mac["macOS Host"]
        Phoenix["Plow.app\n(Swift · Virtualization.framework)"]
        Plowd["plowd daemon\n(:18788 · Python FastAPI)"]

        subgraph VM["Alpine Linux VM"]
            GW["OpenClaw Gateway\n(:18789)"]
            Chan["plow-imessage channel"]
            OC["OpenClaw Agent"]
            CLI["plow CLI\n(containercli)"]
        end
    end

    subgraph Cloud["Cloud (AWS ECS)"]
        API["Plow API\n(FastAPI)"]
        DB["PostgreSQL\n+ pgvector"]
        OE["Opportunity Engine\n(LLM-based JTBD matching)"]

        subgraph Connectors
            GM[Gmail]
            SL[Slack]
        end

        subgraph Channels
            LQ[Linq]
        end
    end

    User --> Phoenix
    Phoenix -->|vsock| VM
    Plowd -->|vsock| VM
    Plowd -->|"gateway operator\n(WebSocket)"| GW
    OC --> CLI
    CLI -->|HTTP| API
    GW --> Chan
    API --> DB
    API --> OE
    API --> GM
    API --> SL
    API --> LQ
```

**Data flow:**

- Inbound SMS → Linq → Plow API webhook → WebSocket → app → LLM → outbound SMS ([full routing doc](docs/architecture/sms-inbound-routing.md))
- `plow gmail search "mortgage"` → CLI → Plow API → Gmail
- `plow slack search "renewal"` → CLI → Plow API → Slack
- OpenClaw never holds raw OAuth tokens — it sees narrow CLI tools only

## Repo Layout

| Directory | Purpose |
|-----------|---------|
| [api/](api/) | Plow API (FastAPI). Connectors in `api/plow/connectors/`, channels in `api/plow/channels/`, opportunity engine in `api/opty_engine/`. |
| [app/](app/) | Plow macOS app (`app/Phoenix/`), plowd daemon (`app/plowd/`), OpenClaw channel plugin (`app/openclaw/`), VM image builder (`app/vm/`). |
| [cli/](cli/) | Plow CLI (containercli). Talks directly to the Plow API via HTTP. |
| [terraform/](terraform/) | AWS infrastructure: ECS, RDS, ALB, VPC, ECR, Secrets Manager. |
| [dtu/](dtu/) | Digital twin units for local testing (e.g., `dtu/linq/` for Linq). |
| [docs/](docs/) | Architecture notes, specs, reports, and spikes. |
| [plow.co/](plow.co/) | Static marketing site deployed on Vercel. |
| [scripts/](scripts/) | Repo-wide utility scripts (e.g., `check_llm_nits.py`). |

## Local Development

The normal local loop is:

1. set up env files
2. boot the API
3. boot the LINQ digital twin
4. build Plow
5. run Plow against the local API

### Prerequisites

- `uv`
- `just`
- Docker Desktop
- Xcode
- macOS for Plow app work

Optional but useful:

- `jq` for inspecting JSON endpoints
- `psql` for local DB inspection
- `npm` if you are changing `app/openclaw/channels/plow-imessage`

### Env files

Each project owns its own env file:

```text
api/.env                 # API runtime config (gitignored) — copy from api/.env.example
api/.env.test-common     # API test defaults (committed)
api/.env.test-live       # API live test creds (gitignored) — copy from api/.env.test-live.example
cli/.env.test-common     # CLI test defaults (committed)
cli/.env.test-live       # CLI live test creds (gitignored) — copy from cli/.env.test-live.example
app/plowd/.env           # Phoenix daemon config (gitignored) — copy from app/plowd/.env.example
dtu/linq/.env            # LINQ twin config (gitignored) — copy from dtu/linq/.env.template
```

### Worktree isolation

`TEST_DB_NAME` is auto-computed from the directory path (`plow_` + 8-char MD5 of the resolved `api/` path). Each checkout/worktree gets a unique test database automatically. CI can override via `TEST_DB_NAME` env var.

For a new worktree:

1. Copy `api/.env.example` to `api/.env`
2. Copy `app/plowd/.env.example` to `app/plowd/.env`
3. Copy `dtu/linq/.env.template` to `dtu/linq/.env`
4. If you want live Gmail or Slack tests, also copy `api/.env.test-live.example` and `cli/.env.test-live.example`

### 1. Set up envs

Create the runtime env files:

```bash
cp api/.env.example api/.env
cp app/plowd/.env.example app/plowd/.env
cp dtu/linq/.env.template dtu/linq/.env
```

`api/.env.example` already defaults LINQ to the local DTU. For most local setups, copying it as-is is enough.

The default local `api/.env` looks like:

```env
DB_HOST=localhost
DB_PORT=55432
DB_NAME=plow
DB_USER=plow
DB_PASSWORD=plow

LINQ_API_KEY=dummy
LINQ_BASE_URL=http://127.0.0.1:8091
LINQ_WEBHOOK_SECRET=test-secret
MANAGED_PHONE=+15550000001

ANTHROPIC_API_KEY=
OAUTH_STATE_SECRET=dev-oauth-state-secret
CORS_ORIGINS='["http://localhost:3000"]'
WEB_BASE_URL=http://localhost:3000
```

Notes:

- LINQ is part of the default local stack. By default the API talks to `dtu/linq`.
- Gmail and Slack OAuth settings are only needed if you want connector flows or live tests.
- `TEST_DB_NAME` is auto-derived for tests. You usually do not need to put it in `api/.env`.
- `just api up` uses the dev DB on port `55432`. The test DB on `55433` is used by test commands.

Minimal `app/plowd/.env` for local app work:

```env
PLOW_API_URL=http://127.0.0.1:8000
```

`dtu/linq/.env` can usually stay close to the template. The important local wiring is:

```env
LINQ_TWIN_PLOW_WEBHOOK_URL=http://127.0.0.1:8000/channels/linq/event
LINQ_TWIN_PLOW_WEBHOOK_SECRET=test-secret
```

If you want live Gmail and Slack tests, also create:

```bash
cp api/.env.test-live.example api/.env.test-live
cp cli/.env.test-live.example cli/.env.test-live
```

### 2. Boot the API

Install Python deps and start the API from the repo root:

```bash
cd api && uv sync
cd ..
just api up
```

What this does:

- starts the shared dev database container if needed
- runs Alembic migrations
- starts the API at `http://127.0.0.1:8000`

Health check:

```bash
curl http://127.0.0.1:8000/health
```

### 3. Boot the LINQ twin

From the repo root:

```bash
cd dtu/linq && just sync
cd ../..
just dtu linq up
```

Then open:

```text

exec
/bin/bash -lc "find docs -maxdepth 2 \\( -iname '*architecture*' -o -iname 'arc*.md' -o -name 'phoenix.md' \\) -print | sort" in /tmp/tmp.uqndeHEwzr/repo
 succeeded in 0ms:
docs/architecture
docs/architecture/openclaw-phoenix-architecture.md
docs/phoenix-openclaw-security-architecture.md
docs/phoenix.md

/bin/bash -lc "sed -n '1,260p' docs/phoenix.md" in /tmp/tmp.uqndeHEwzr/repo

# Plow App

The Plow macOS app is currently a local OpenClaw wrapper/orchestrator centered on **PlowD** (the daemon).

## Current Focus

As of this branch, the primary path is:

- PlowD starts and manages OpenClaw gateway + node host locally.
- Plow app tooling keeps app-managed config/state under `~/Library/Application Support/co.plow.app/...` instead of `~/.openclaw/...`.
- Plow gateway policy/operator logic handles pairing and tool approval flows.

The older Wren/Inbox agent-first flow is not the main focus right now, and agent registration on daemon startup is currently disabled.

## Architecture (Now)

- **PlowD (FastAPI daemon)**: process manager + policy service + status API.
- **OpenClaw gateway**: local websocket gateway (`127.0.0.1`, token auth).
- **OpenClaw node host**: execution host process paired to the gateway.
- **OpenClaw dashboard/web chat**: optional local UI client that connects to the same gateway.
- **Plow macOS app**: still exists as iMessage bridge/UI layer, but not the center of this PR.

OpenClaw terms vs app terms:

- PlowD == daemon/operator-side process (not the gateway binary itself)
- OpenClaw gateway == websocket service PlowD launches/manages
- Web client/dashboard == separate operator client that can connect locally when paired/authenticated

State roots are bundle-scoped:

- production app: `~/Library/Application Support/co.plow.app/`
- default dev app: `~/Library/Application Support/co.plow.app.dev/`
- worktree/dev variants: `~/Library/Application Support/<bundle-id>/`

## Quick Start

For the full local stack, use the root [README.md](../README.md). This document stays focused on app-specific details.

1. Set up daemon env:

```bash
cd app/plowd
uv sync
cp .env.example .env
```

2. Fill `app/plowd/.env` with at least:

```env
PLOW_API_URL=http://127.0.0.1:8000
```

3. Build the app bundle and packaged runtime:

```bash
just app build
```

4. Run Plow against the local API:

```bash
just app up
```

## Dev Mode (Fast Iteration)

Use this when you want Python hot reload and live OpenClaw asset edits from the repo:

```bash
just app build
just app up
```

`just app build` prepares the app bundle, including the VM image and packaged runtime. `just app up` is then the normal fast loop for Swift changes: it rebuilds the app and launches with PlowD from `app/plowd/plowd` using `uvicorn --reload`. Run `just app build` again after changes under `app/plowd/`, `app/vm/`, or packaged OpenClaw/runtime assets.

For channel TypeScript edits under `app/openclaw/channels/plow-imessage`:

```bash
cd app/openclaw/channels/plow-imessage && npm run build:watch
```

## Daily Commands

```bash
just app build                     # build Swift app + package VM/runtime into the current dev app variant
just app up
just app release                   # signed release package under .build/

just app logs                      # multiplexed app/PlowD/app-vm/openclaw logs
```

Workflow split:

- run the repo from the root `justfile`
- use `app/justfile` only for local Swift app commands
- use `app/plowd/justfile` only for local daemon tests and internal maintenance; the supported app workflows are the `just app ...` commands above

## Code Signing

Local app builds are signed by default with the shared Apple team configured in [`app/Phoenix/Config/App.xcconfig`](../app/Phoenix/Config/App.xcconfig). That keeps dev builds closer to production identity and is the preferred path for avoiding repeated Full Disk Access prompts during normal rebuilds.

For release notarization setup and the personal-team override path, see [`docs/distribution/CODE_SIGNING.md`](distribution/CODE_SIGNING.md).

## State Boundaries (Avoiding Sprawl)

OpenClaw runs inside a VM sandbox managed by PlowD. All state is pinned to app-managed paths under `~/Library/Application Support/<bundle-id>/`:

- config: `~/Library/Application Support/<bundle-id>/openclaw/gateway/openclaw.json`
- state: `~/Library/Application Support/<bundle-id>/openclaw/gateway`
- token: `~/Library/Application Support/<bundle-id>/openclaw/secrets/gateway-token`

Reference and recovery notes:

- [plowd/README.md](../app/plowd/README.md)
- [openclaw-phoenixd-known-behaviors.md](openclaw-phoenixd-known-behaviors.md)

## macOS Layer (Still Present)

The Swift app in `app/` still provides the iMessage bridge (`chat.db` clone to `~/Library/Application Support/<bundle-id>/imessage/chat_clone.db`) and local UI controls. `app/plowd/` is the host daemon/runtime. That layer remains part of the Plow app, but this PR/workstream is primarily about stable OpenClaw gateway integration through PlowD.

## Repository Layout

```
plow/
├── api/                  # Plow cloud API (FastAPI, SQLAlchemy, connectors)
├── app/                  # Swift app, PlowD daemon, OpenClaw assets, VM
│   ├── Phoenix/          # Swift macOS app
│   ├── plowd/            # Python FastAPI daemon
│   ├── openclaw/         # Channel plugins (plow-imessage)
│   └── vm/               # VM sandbox: rootfs build, kernel, init scripts
├── cli/                  # Plow CLI (runs inside OpenClaw container)
├── dtu/                  # Digital twin units for local testing
├── docs/                 # Architecture docs, spikes, distribution guides
└── justfile              # Primary command entrypoints
```

# Phoenix + OpenClaw Security Architecture (Current State and Hardening Roadmap)

Last updated: 2026-03-03

## Purpose

This document describes:
1. The architecture Phoenix is running today.
2. What security guarantees exist right now.
3. What is not guaranteed yet.
4. A phased hardening roadmap.

This is a living architecture document for implementation and product/security decisions.

## System Overview (Current)

Core components:
1. `Phoenix.app` (macOS UI client).
2. `phoenixd` (local policy and orchestration daemon).
3. `openclaw-gateway` (tool runtime and agent loop, `exec.host = "gateway"` inside Alpine VM).
4. `phoenix-gateway-guard` OpenClaw plugin (`before_tool_call` / `after_tool_call` hooks).

Current runtime shape:
1. Gateway is bound to loopback (`127.0.0.1`) with token auth.
2. PhoenixD manages OpenClaw config reconciliation and process supervision.
3. PhoenixD resolves exec approval events over gateway operator websocket.
4. Tool policy decisions are requested from PhoenixD by plugin hook callbacks.

## Trust Boundaries (Current)

1. UI does not need direct gateway token by design.
2. Gateway token is stored in user home (`~/.phoenix/openclaw/secrets/gateway-token`) with mode `0600`.
3. Gateway/API access is local-only by bind setting, but same-user processes remain in the trust boundary.
4. OpenClaw runtime (gateway + node host) is currently trusted to honor plugin/approval contracts.

## Request Flow (Current)

Non-exec tool call:
1. OpenClaw gateway receives tool request.
2. Plugin `before_tool_call` calls PhoenixD `/internal/gateway/policy/tool-check`.
3. If denied or unavailable, plugin blocks (fail closed).
4. If allowed, OpenClaw gateway executes the tool.
5. Plugin `after_tool_call` posts result to PhoenixD audit endpoint.

Exec tool call:
1. Same `before_tool_call` policy gate applies.
2. Gateway raises `exec.approval.requested`.
3. PhoenixD operator client approves or denies.
4. If approved, execution happens via OpenClaw node host (`system.run`), not inside PhoenixD process.

## What Is Secure Today

1. Default local exposure is reduced (`loopback` + token auth).
2. Policy checks for tool calls are centralized in PhoenixD (plugin gate).
3. Exec has a second approval layer (`ask=always`, fallback deny behavior).
4. PhoenixD owns config reconciliation and can enforce baseline guardrails.
5. Operational audit trail exists for tool checks/results and process status.

## What Is Not Guaranteed Yet

1. Full runtime isolation from OpenClaw bugs is not guaranteed.
2. Gateway network egress is not fully forced through Phoenix today.
3. Same-user token theft is still possible on a compromised local account.
4. OpenClaw runtime remains a trusted enforcement participant in current model.
5. OS-level sandbox boundaries are not yet applied to gateway/node runtime paths.
6. `agentId` is not currently a cryptographically attested identity primitive and should not be used as a sole authorization key.
7. Plugin->Phoenix policy callbacks (`/internal/gateway/policy/*`) are local but currently do not have an additional dedicated auth secret/signature layer.

## Principal Risks

1. OpenClaw bypass bug risk:
   - A severe runtime bug could bypass plugin/approval paths.
2. Gateway direct egress risk:
   - Gateway may call model/provider/channels directly unless egress is constrained.
3. Same-user token risk:
   - Any process running as the same macOS user can potentially read the gateway token file.
4. Control-plane surface risk:
   - Any client with token and loopback access can attempt privileged gateway operations.
5. Identity confusion risk:
   - Policy decisions based only on payload-level `agentId` can be weaker than decisions keyed by stronger session/channel context.

## Identity Signals and Trust Strength (Current)

Signals currently observed in exec approval payloads include `agentId`, `sessionKey`, `nodeId`, and `systemRunBindingV1`.

Current trust guidance:
1. Treat `agentId` as a UX/debug attribute, not the sole security principal.
2. Treat `(nodeId, sessionKey)` as the primary policy key in current architecture.
3. Enforce consistency checks across duplicate fields (for example, `request.sessionKey` and `request.systemRunBindingV1.sessionKey`) and deny on mismatch.
4. Preserve fail-closed behavior for ambiguous or missing identity fields.

## Hardening Roadmap

### Phase 0: Baseline discipline (now)

1. Keep loopback bind and token auth mandatory.
2. Keep plugin required and fail-closed for policy check.
3. Keep exec ask/approval as deny-by-default fallback.
4. Keep OpenClaw version pinning and deterministic config reconciliation.
5. Prefer policy mode `ask` after approval UI is live.

### Phase 1: UX and approval authority hardening

1. Add PhoenixD <-> app websocket channel for real-time approvals.
2. Move default approval mode from auto-allow to ask.
3. Improve retry behavior around first exec/pairing race.
4. Add token rotation command and operational runbook.
5. Add explicit principal mapping and mismatch-deny logic keyed by `(nodeId, sessionKey)`.

### Phase 2: Packaging and deterministic runtime

1. Bundle pinned Node + OpenClaw runtime in signed installer payload.
2. Remove end-user npm/pnpm install dependencies.
3. Ensure daemon startup and bootstrap are deterministic on fresh machine.
4. Migrate startup ownership to launchd/SMAppService model.
5. Add plugin->Phoenix policy endpoint authentication (shared secret and/or signed request envelope).

### Phase 3: Execution isolation

1. Introduce Phoenix-managed sandboxed executor helper for exec path.
2. Route approved exec through sandbox helper instead of broad host runtime.
3. Enforce file/network/process limits at OS sandbox boundary.

### Phase 4: Egress and runtime containment

1. Enforce proxy-only provider/network path where gateway cannot bypass Phoenix proxy.
2. Add strong egress controls for gateway runtime.
3. Move toward full gateway/node isolation (containerized boundary or VM).
4. Optionally adopt custom Phoenix node runtime if protocol maintenance cost is acceptable.

## Strategic Options and Tradeoffs

Option A: Keep official OpenClaw node host + sandboxed exec helper
1. Lower maintenance cost.
2. Faster path to meaningful hardening.
3. Still trusts OpenClaw for non-exec behavior unless further containment is added.

Option B: Build custom Phoenix node runtime
1. Stronger control over exec enforcement.
2. Higher long-term protocol compatibility and maintenance burden.
3. Best used after installer and sandbox foundations are stable.

Option C: Full runtime containment (VM-level)
1. Strongest isolation model.
2. Highest complexity and operational cost.
3. Appropriate for high-assurance security target.

## Decision Guidance

Recommended near-term order:
1. Finish approval websocket/UI path.
2. Ship deterministic installer/runtime packaging.
3. Implement sandboxed exec helper.
4. Add proxy-only egress enforcement.
5. Reassess need for custom node runtime after measuring residual risk.

## Open Questions

1. What minimum security bar is required for first public installer release?
2. Is same-user token storage acceptable until Keychain migration, or should Keychain be mandatory before release?
3. Do we treat non-exec gateway egress lock-down as release-blocking or phase 2 hardening?
4. At what point does custom node runtime ROI exceed maintenance cost?

# OpenClaw + Phoenix Architecture

*Last updated: 2026-03-04*

> **Historical.** This document describes the pre-Lima architecture: a custom Alpine
> rootfs booted via `Virtualization.framework` with vsock/socat bridges. On branch
> `vm/lima-migration`, OpenClaw now runs as an OCI container inside a Lima-managed
> Debian guest (`app/vm/lima.yaml`, `app/Phoenix/LimaVMManager.swift`), and the
> rootfs/initramfs/vsock machinery below is gone. Gatekeeper (Camoufox) still uses
> `Virtualization.framework` on this branch. See PR #310 for the Lima migration
> (openclaw + Debian guest) and the camoufox-on-lima follow-up.

---

## Ownership Map

Every component in this system is owned by exactly one of two codebases:

| Owner | Codebase | Language | What It Owns |
|-------|----------|----------|-------------|
| **OpenClaw** (upstream) | `openclaw/upstream` | Node.js / TypeScript | Gateway, Plugin SDK, Channels, Heartbeat, Cron, ACP, Sessions, Control UI, Pi agent runner |
| **Phoenix** (ours) | `app/`, `app/plowd/`, `app/vm/` | Python + Swift + JS plugins | PhoenixD daemon, macOS app, VM image, phoenix-gateway-guard plugin, plow-imessage channel plugin, config generation |

**Phoenix owns zero lines of the Gateway.** We treat OpenClaw as an opaque
runtime binary. Our integration surface is:

1. **VM management** — we run `openclaw gateway run` inside an Alpine Linux VM (Virtualization.framework)
2. **WebSocket operator protocol** — we connect as an operator to observe and approve
3. **Plugin hooks** — our JS plugin runs inside their Gateway process
4. **Channel plugin** — our plow-imessage channel plugin runs inside their Gateway process
5. **Config generation** — we write `openclaw.json` with locked guardrails

**Note:** We use `exec.host = "gateway"` — the Gateway executes commands directly
(no separate Node Host process). The VM itself provides sandboxing.

---

## System Overview

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                          USER / CLIENT TIER                             │
│                                                                         │
│  ┌──────────┐  ┌──────────┐  ┌──────────────┐  ┌────────────────────┐  │
│  │ macOS App│  │ CLI      │  │ Control UI   │  │ Channels           │  │
│  │ (Swift)  │  │ `openclaw│  │ (Web Dashboard│  │ iMessage (Plow),   │  │
│  │          │  │  agent`  │  │  served by   │  │ Telegram, Discord, │  │
│  │  PHOENIX │  │ OPENCLAW │  │  Gateway)    │  │ Slack, WhatsApp,   │  │
│  │          │  │          │  │  OPENCLAW    │  │ etc.               │  │
│  └────┬─────┘  └────┬─────┘  └──────┬───────┘  │  OPENCLAW          │  │
│       │              │               │          └────────┬───────────┘  │

        │              │               │                   │
        │  WebSocket   │  WebSocket    │  WebSocket        │  WebSocket/
        │  :18788/ws   │  :18789       │  :18789           │  Webhooks
        ▼              ▼               ▼                   ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐

│  │                                 │  │ (WebSocket → Gateway :18789) │  │

│  │ POST /internal/.../tool-result  │  │ Scopes:                      │  │
│  │ GET  /status                    │  │   operator.approvals         │  │
│  │ POST /activate (channel setup)  │  │   operator.pairing           │  │
│  │ WS   /ws (approval UI socket)  │  │                              │  │
│  └──────────┬──────────────────────┘  │ Handles:                     │  │
│             │                         │   exec.approval.requested    │  │
│  ┌──────────▼──────────────────────┐  │   node.pair.requested        │  │

│  │ evaluate_tool_call()  → allow/  │  │ Resolves:                    │  │
│  │ evaluate_exec_approval() deny   │  │   exec.approval.resolve      │  │
│  │ record_tool_result()  → audit   │  │   node.pair.approve/reject   │  │
│  ├─────────────────────────────────┤  └──────────────────────────────┘  │
│  │ ApprovalStore            ★ OURS │                                    │
│  │                                 │                                    │
│  │ policy_mode: ask|allow|deny     │                                    │
│  │ pending approvals queue         │                                    │
│  │ 120s timeout → auto-deny       │                                    │
│  │ broadcasts to WS /ws clients   │                                    │
│  └─────────────────────────────────┘                                    │
└──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┬──────────────┘
                          Manages ▼ (inside VM)
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│  ALPINE LINUX VM (Virtualization.framework)                             │
│  exec.host = "gateway" — no separate node process, VM is the sandbox   │
│                                                                         │
│  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │
│  │ OpenClaw Gateway ★ UPSTREAM (Node.js)                              │ │
│  │ 127.0.0.1:18789                                                    │ │
│  │                                                                    │ │
│  │ WebSocket + HTTP server                                            │ │
│  │ Agent sessions, tool execution, channels, heartbeat, cron          │ │
│  │                                                                    │ │
│  │ Loaded plugins:                                                    │ │
│  │   phoenix-gateway-guard ★ OURS — tool policy callbacks to PhoenixD │ │
│  │   plow-imessage ★ OURS — iMessage channel via Plow cloud service   │ │
│  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │
│                                                                         │
│  socat bridges: vsock ↔ TCP (transparent to both sides)                │
│  virtiofs mounts: workspace, config, mounts, plugin, channel, vm-logs  │
│  NAT networking: outbound internet via VZNATNetworkDeviceAttachment     │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

---

## VM Sandbox Architecture

### Why a VM?

- **Isolation**: The Gateway executes arbitrary shell commands on behalf of agents
  (`exec.host = "gateway"`). Running inside a VM limits blast radius — a compromised
  agent cannot access the host filesystem, keychain, or other processes.
- **Reproducibility**: The Alpine rootfs is built deterministically via Docker. Every
  install gets the same sandbox environment.
- **Transparency**: vsock + socat bridges make the VM boundary invisible to both
  PhoenixD and OpenClaw. Gateway still binds `127.0.0.1:18789`, PhoenixD still connects
  to that port — the bridges handle the translation.

### Boot Chain

```
VZLinuxBootLoader (direct kernel boot, no UEFI/bootloader)
  vmlinuz (Alpine linux-virt kernel)
  initramfs.img (custom, static busybox)
    → load kernel modules: virtio, ext4, vsock, virtiofs, virtio_net
    → mount /dev/vda (ext4 rootfs)
    → switch_root

  /sbin/init (PID 1, shell script)
    → mount /proc, /sys, /dev
    → ip link set lo up
    → bring up NAT interface (eth0) via DHCP
    → set deterministic DNS (8.8.8.8, 1.1.1.1)
    → mount virtiofs shares
    → bind-mount /vm-logs → /tmp/openclaw (shared log access)
    → start socat bridges (A + B)
    → signal host readiness via vsock :18787
    → wait for PhoenixD to seed /config/gateway/openclaw.json + gateway token
    → start openclaw-start.sh (gateway)
    → wait for gateway readiness on :18789 (up to 30s)
    → wait (PID 1 keeps running)
```

### Bridge Architecture

Four bridges connect host and guest. Guest-side bridges are socat (in init script).
Host-side bridges are Swift (in VMManager.swift) using NWListener and VZVirtioSocketDevice.

```
HOST (macOS)                                 VM (Alpine Linux)

Bridge C (Swift, VMManager)                  Bridge A (socat, init)
  vsock :18788 listener ◄────vsock────►  TCP-LISTEN:18788 → VSOCK-CONNECT:2:18788
  → relay to TCP 127.0.0.1:18788             (plugin HTTP → PhoenixD)

Bridge D (Swift, VMManager)                  Bridge B (socat, init)
  NWListener TCP :18789 ────vsock────►  VSOCK-LISTEN:18789 → TCP:127.0.0.1:18789
  → relay to vsock :18789                    (PhoenixD → Gateway)
```

**Traffic flows:**
1. PhoenixD (host :18788) ← Bridge C ← vsock ← Bridge A ← plugin HTTP (guest)
2. PhoenixD (host) → TCP :18789 → Bridge D → vsock → Bridge B → Gateway :18789 (guest)

Port 18787 is used only for the one-shot readiness signal (guest → host).

### VM Networking

The VM has outbound internet via `VZNATNetworkDeviceAttachment` (Apple's built-in NAT).
This is needed for channel plugins (e.g., plow-imessage WebSocket to `api.plow.co`).

**Requirements for outbound to work:**
- `virtio_net` kernel module loaded in initramfs (plus `failover`, `net_failover`)
- NAT interface brought up via `ip link set eth0 up` + `udhcpc` in init
- Deterministic DNS written to `/etc/resolv.conf` (DHCP DNS is unreliable)
- `ca-certificates` installed in the rootfs (for HTTPS)

### virtiofs Mounts

| Tag | Guest Path | Mode | Purpose |
|-----|-----------|------|---------|
| workspace | /workspace | rw | Agent working directory |
| config | /config | rw | Gateway config, secrets, sessions, agents |
| mounts | /mounts | rw | Additional host paths |
| plugin | /plugin | ro | phoenix-gateway-guard plugin (from app bundle) |
| channel | /plow-imessage | ro | plow-imessage channel plugin (from app bundle) |
| vm-logs | /vm-logs | rw | Shared log directory (bind-mounted to /tmp/openclaw) |
| desktop | /host/Desktop | rw | Host user's Desktop (when mounted) |

Plugin and channel dirs are compiled JS from the app bundle, mounted read-only.
Config must be rw — the Gateway writes `openclaw.json`, sessions, and secrets.
Host-share paths are strict: `/host/<Name>` exists only when the corresponding
share is actually mounted. The guest does not keep placeholder directories for
absent shares. Updating the share set requires a VM restart.
Phoenix JSON-encodes the active `[HostShare]` list into the
`PLOWD_OPENCLAW_ACTIVE_HOST_SHARES` environment variable before spawning
plowd; plowd reads that env var as its sole source of truth for which
host shares are live this session.

### rootfs Management

The rootfs image is copied from the signed app bundle to `~/.phoenix/vm/rootfs.img`
(writable location required by Virtualization.framework). VMManager auto-detects
bundle changes by comparing file sizes — if the bundle rootfs differs from the local
copy, it replaces the local copy on next boot. No manual deletion needed.

### VM Image Build

3-stage Dockerfile in `app/vm/build-vm-image`:

1. **builder** — Alpine + Node.js + `npm install -g openclaw@<version>`
2. **runtime** — Clean Alpine with `linux-virt` kernel, socat, Node.js, ca-certificates.
   Copies node_modules from builder. Copies init scripts.
3. **initramfs-builder** — Builds custom initramfs from runtime's kernel modules
   (static busybox, decompressed `.ko` files, custom init script)

Outputs: `app/vm/output/{vmlinuz, initramfs.img, rootfs.img}`

**Why custom initramfs?** Alpine's stock `initramfs-virt` uses OpenRC — incompatible
with Virtualization.framework's direct kernel boot. Our initramfs uses static busybox
and loads only the modules we need.

---

## Channel Plugin: plow-imessage

The `plow-imessage` channel plugin connects the OpenClaw agent to iMessage via the
Plow cloud service (`api.plow.co`). It runs inside the Gateway process as an
OpenClaw channel plugin.

```
iMessage user
    ↓ (Apple iMessage)
Plow Cloud Service (api.plow.co)
    ↓ (WebSocket, wss://api.plow.co/ws)
plow-imessage channel plugin (inside VM)
    ↓ (OpenClaw dispatch pipeline)
OpenClaw Gateway → Agent → LLM → response
    ↓ (deliver callback)
plow-imessage → Plow API → iMessage user
```

**Key files:**
- `app/openclaw/channels/plow-imessage/src/channel.ts` — plugin definition, inbound dispatch
- `app/openclaw/channels/plow-imessage/src/ws-client.ts` — WebSocket client with auth + reconnect
- `app/openclaw/channels/plow-imessage/src/send.ts` — outbound message API
- `app/openclaw/channels/plow-imessage/src/config.ts` — account config adapter
- `app/openclaw/channels/plow-imessage/src/runtime.ts` — OpenClaw runtime accessor

**Build:** `cd app/openclaw/channels/plow-imessage && npm run build` (tsc), then `just build`
to package into app bundle. The compiled JS is mounted into the VM via virtiofs.

**Activation:** The Swift app calls `POST /activations/create` on the cloud API, opens
Messages with `Plow Activate: <code>`, then polls `POST /activations/redeem`. On
success, the app writes a pending token file that PhoenixD consumes at startup to

   512	    private func updateWindowHeight(for screen: InstallerScreen) {
   513	        guard let window = NSApp.windows.first(where: { $0.identifier?.rawValue == "installer" }) else { return }
   514	        let targetHeight = targetWindowHeight(for: screen)
   515	        let currentFrame = window.frame
   516	        guard abs(currentFrame.height - targetHeight) > 1 else { return }
   517	        let newOriginY = currentFrame.origin.y + (currentFrame.height - targetHeight)
   518	        let newFrame = NSRect(
   519	            x: currentFrame.origin.x,
   520	            y: newOriginY,
   521	            width: currentFrame.width,
   522	            height: targetHeight
   523	        )
   524	        NSAnimationContext.runAnimationGroup { context in
   525	            context.duration = 0.45
   526	            context.timingFunction = CAMediaTimingFunction(name: .easeOut)
   527	            window.animator().setFrame(newFrame, display: true)
   528	        }
   529	    }
   530	
   531	    /// The connectors screen sizes its window to the measured content height
   532	    /// (reported via `InstallerContentHeightKey`), capped by the visible
   533	    /// screen so a long settings list falls back to scrolling inside the
   534	    /// existing `ScrollView` instead of pushing the window off-screen. The
   535	    /// remaining screens are intentionally taller than their content
   536	    /// (Spacer-filled splash layouts) and keep the design height of 530.
   537	    private func targetWindowHeight(for screen: InstallerScreen) -> CGFloat {
   538	        let cap = max(420, (NSScreen.main?.visibleFrame.height ?? 900) - 80)
   539	        if screen == .connectors, measuredContentHeight > 0 {
   540	            // `measuredContentHeight` is the ScrollView's inner content; add
   541	            // the window's titlebar plus the bottom-anchored DownloadBarView's
   542	            // measured height (zero when the bar isn't shown) so the bar
   543	            // doesn't cover the last settings rows.
   544	            return min(measuredContentHeight + 28 + measuredOverlayHeight, cap)
   545	        }
   546	        return 530
   547	    }
   548	}
   549	
   550	/// Carries the bottom-anchored `DownloadBarView`'s measured height up to
   551	/// `InstallerView` so the connectors-screen window can grow to keep the
   552	/// settings content above the bar (including the taller "retry" state).
   553	/// Reverts to zero when the bar isn't in the view tree (post-install).
   554	struct InstallerOverlayHeightKey: PreferenceKey {
   555	    static var defaultValue: CGFloat = 0
   556	    static func reduce(value: inout CGFloat, nextValue: () -> CGFloat) {
   557	        value = nextValue()
   558	    }
   559	}
   560	
   561	/// SwiftUI preference key carrying the measured intrinsic height of the
   562	/// active installer screen up to `InstallerView`, which drives the hosting
   563	/// `NSWindow`'s frame. Emitted by `SettingsView` (the only screen whose
   564	/// content can grow as connectors/containers come and go); unspecified for
   565	/// the splash screens, which use a design-set fixed height.
   566	struct InstallerContentHeightKey: PreferenceKey {
   567	    static var defaultValue: CGFloat = 0
   568	    static func reduce(value: inout CGFloat, nextValue: () -> CGFloat) {
   569	        value = nextValue()
   570	    }

     1	import Foundation
     2	import IOKit.pwr_mgt
     3	import Observation
     4	
     5	/// Holds `kIOPMAssertionTypePreventSystemSleep` while the user has opted in, so
     6	/// the system stays in Dark Wake and the OpenClaw VM keeps serving through
     7	/// display sleep. On battery the kernel effectively downgrades the assertion to
     8	/// idle-sleep-prevention, so a single assertion works in both power states;
     9	/// draining the battery is the user's explicit choice when they toggle this on.
    10	/// Clamshell sleep on a MacBook without an external display is still enforced
    11	/// by the kernel and isn't reachable from a public API.
    12	@Observable
    13	@MainActor
    14	final class KeepMacAwake {
    15	    static let preferencesKey = "keepMacAwakeWhileRunning"
    16	
    17	    private var assertionID: IOPMAssertionID = 0
    18	    private var hasAssertion = false
    19	    /// Set true while we're rewriting `isEnabled` from inside `applyEnabled` to
    20	    /// revert a failed acquire — the recursive `didSet` reads this and bails.
    21	    private var suppressApply = false
    22	
    23	    var isEnabled: Bool = UserDefaults.standard.bool(forKey: KeepMacAwake.preferencesKey) {
    24	        didSet { applyEnabled() }
    25	    }
    26	
    27	    init() {
    28	        applyEnabled()
    29	    }
    30	
    31	    /// Releases the assertion deterministically before the app exits, so the
    32	    /// system can coalesce any pending sleep state at logout/shutdown without
    33	    /// waiting for process reaping. The kernel reclaims the assertion on
    34	    /// process exit either way; this is a courtesy, not a leak fix.
    35	    func teardown() {
    36	        releaseAssertion()
    37	    }
    38	
    39	    /// Brings the held IOPMAssertion in line with `isEnabled` and persists the
    40	    /// resolved value. If acquire fails, `isEnabled` is reverted to false so
    41	    /// the toggle UI matches the actual sleep-prevention state.
    42	    private func applyEnabled() {
    43	        guard !suppressApply else { return }
    44	        if isEnabled {
    45	            if !acquireAssertion() {
    46	                suppressApply = true
    47	                isEnabled = false
    48	                suppressApply = false
    49	            }
    50	        } else {
    51	            releaseAssertion()
    52	        }
    53	        UserDefaults.standard.set(isEnabled, forKey: Self.preferencesKey)
    54	    }
    55	
    56	    private func acquireAssertion() -> Bool {
    57	        guard !hasAssertion else { return true }
    58	        var id: IOPMAssertionID = 0
    59	        let reason = "Plow keeping Mac awake so the OpenClaw VM stays reachable" as CFString
    60	        let result = IOPMAssertionCreateWithName(
    61	            kIOPMAssertionTypePreventSystemSleep as CFString,
    62	            IOPMAssertionLevel(kIOPMAssertionLevelOn),
    63	            reason,
    64	            &id
    65	        )
    66	        guard result == kIOReturnSuccess else {
    67	            NSLog("[KeepMacAwake] IOPMAssertionCreateWithName failed: %d", result)
    68	            return false
    69	        }
    70	        assertionID = id
    71	        hasAssertion = true
    72	        return true
    73	    }
    74	
    75	    private func releaseAssertion() {
    76	        guard hasAssertion else { return }
    77	        IOPMAssertionRelease(assertionID)
    78	        hasAssertion = false
    79	        assertionID = 0
    80	    }
    81	}

   108	    /// Fires when the user tries to "open" Plow while it's already running:
   109	    /// Dock-tile click, Finder double-click, `open -a Plow`, Spotlight. This is
   110	    /// the moment to guarantee the UI is reachable — the menu bar button may
   111	    /// be hidden behind the notch, pushed off-screen by overflow, or tucked
   112	    /// away by Bartender, and the user just told us they want to see the app.
   113	    func applicationShouldHandleReopen(_ sender: NSApplication, hasVisibleWindows flag: Bool) -> Bool {
   114	        if installerController.isVisible {
   115	            installerController.show(downloadManager: downloadManager, client: daemonClient, keepMacAwake: keepMacAwake)
   116	        } else {
   117	            menuBarController?.showPanel()
   118	        }
   119	        return true
   120	    }

   140	    func show(downloadManager: RuntimeDownloadManager, client: DaemonClient, keepMacAwake: KeepMacAwake) {
   141	        if let window {
   142	            NSApp.setActivationPolicy(.regular)
   143	            window.makeKeyAndOrderFront(nil)
   144	            NSRunningApplication.current.activate(options: [.activateAllWindows, .activateIgnoringOtherApps])

   157	            }
   158	        )
   159	        let hostingView = NSHostingView(rootView: view)
   160	
   161	        NSApp.setActivationPolicy(.regular)
   162	
   163	        let window = NSWindow(
   164	            contentRect: .zero,
   165	            styleMask: [.titled, .closable, .fullSizeContentView],
   166	            backing: .buffered,
   167	            defer: false
   168	        )
   169	        window.contentView = hostingView
   170	        window.setContentSize(NSSize(width: 680, height: 530))
   171	        window.backgroundColor = NSColor(red: 0.07, green: 0.08, blue: 0.07, alpha: 1)
   172	        // Pin the window (and any NSPopover it hosts) to dark appearance so popovers/alerts
   173	        // don't render with light system material when the user is in Light Mode.
   174	        window.appearance = NSAppearance(named: .darkAqua)
   175	        window.titlebarAppearsTransparent = true
   176	        window.titleVisibility = .hidden
   177	        window.identifier = NSUserInterfaceItemIdentifier("installer")
   178	        window.isMovableByWindowBackground = true
   179	        window.center()
   180	        window.delegate = self

Subject: [PATCH 01/12] fix(phoenix): apply review fixes to keep-mac-awake
 feature

- Mark KeepMacAwake @MainActor and convert isEnabled to a stored
  @Observable property so off-screen observers re-render on toggle.
- Log IOPMAssertionCreateWithName failures so a stuck UserDefaults=ON
  state doesn't go silent.
- Move "Keep Mac Awake" out of the Connectors card into its own
  System section.
- Rewrite teardown() doc to drop the false "kernel leak" claim;
  IOPMAssertions are reaped on process exit either way.

+            if isEnabled {
                 acquireAssertion()

@@ -34,6 +34,14 @@ final class KeepMacAwake {
         }
     }
 
+    /// Releases the assertion deterministically before the app exits, so the
+    /// system can coalesce any pending sleep state at logout/shutdown without
+    /// waiting for process reaping. The kernel reclaims the assertion on
+    /// process exit either way; this is a courtesy, not a leak fix.
+    func teardown() {
+        releaseAssertion()
+    }
+
     private func acquireAssertion() {

From 74908cacaccf49112d9213f730cd24029f78470c Mon Sep 17 00:00:00 2001
From: Swagat Patel <swagatpatel@users.noreply.github.com>
Date: Thu, 30 Apr 2026 14:55:21 -0700
Subject: [PATCH 02/12] fix(phoenix): revert KeepMacAwake toggle when assertion
 acquire fails

If IOPMAssertionCreateWithName returns non-success, isEnabled is now
flipped back to false (and persisted) so the UI no longer shows a
"keep awake" state that the kernel didn't grant. didSet recursion is
gated by a `suppressApply` flag so the revert can't loop.

-    private func acquireAssertion() {

+    /// Brings the held IOPMAssertion in line with `isEnabled` and persists the
+    /// resolved value. If acquire fails, `isEnabled` is reverted to false so
+    /// the toggle UI matches the actual sleep-prevention state.
+    private func applyEnabled() {
+        guard !suppressApply else { return }
+        if isEnabled {
+            if !acquireAssertion() {
+                suppressApply = true
+                isEnabled = false
+                suppressApply = false
+            }
+        } else {
+            releaseAssertion()
+        }
+        UserDefaults.standard.set(isEnabled, forKey: Self.preferencesKey)
+    }
+
+    private func acquireAssertion() -> Bool {
+        guard !hasAssertion else { return true }
         var id: IOPMAssertionID = 0
         let reason = "Plow keeping Mac awake so the OpenClaw VM stays reachable" as CFString
         let result = IOPMAssertionCreateWithName(

+            return false
         }
         assertionID = id
         hasAssertion = true
+        return true
     }

From c06d059ebde64258a8c22cc40f38b7730d22e2a3 Mon Sep 17 00:00:00 2001
From: Swagat Patel <swagatpatel@users.noreply.github.com>
Date: Thu, 30 Apr 2026 14:55:29 -0700
Subject: [PATCH 03/12] fix(phoenix): auto-size installer window to settings
 content

Replace the hard-coded 530/610 window heights with a SwiftUI preference
key (InstallerContentHeightKey) that propagates SettingsView's measured
content height up to InstallerView. The hosting NSWindow resizes to fit
that height, capped by visibleFrame - 80 so the existing ScrollView
still acts as a fallback when content genuinely exceeds the screen.

Splash screens (welcomeInstall, FDA, activation, connectContext) keep
the design-set 530 since their layouts rely on Spacer fill, not
intrinsic content height.

Co-Authored-By: Claude Opus 4.7 (1M context) <noreply@anthropic.com>

+    /// The connectors screen sizes its window to the measured content height
+    /// (reported via `InstallerContentHeightKey`), capped by the visible
+    /// screen so a long settings list falls back to scrolling inside the
+    /// existing `ScrollView` instead of pushing the window off-screen. The
+    /// remaining screens are intentionally taller than their content
+    /// (Spacer-filled splash layouts) and keep the design height of 530.
+    private func targetWindowHeight(for screen: InstallerScreen) -> CGFloat {
+        let cap = max(420, (NSScreen.main?.visibleFrame.height ?? 900) - 80)
+        if screen == .connectors, measuredContentHeight > 0 {
+            // `measuredContentHeight` is the ScrollView's inner content; add
+            // the window's titlebar so the visible area matches.
+            return min(measuredContentHeight + 28, cap)
+        }
+        return 530
+    }
+}
+
+/// SwiftUI preference key carrying the measured intrinsic height of the
+/// active installer screen up to `InstallerView`, which drives the hosting
+/// `NSWindow`'s frame. Emitted by `SettingsView` (the only screen whose
+/// content can grow as connectors/containers come and go); unspecified for
+/// the splash screens, which use a design-set fixed height.
+struct InstallerContentHeightKey: PreferenceKey {
+    static var defaultValue: CGFloat = 0
+    static func reduce(value: inout CGFloat, nextValue: () -> CGFloat) {
+        value = nextValue()
+    }
 }

From ad4e121fc43e8f99402f48a7bdace8623b498e24 Mon Sep 17 00:00:00 2001
From: Swagat Patel <swagatpatel@users.noreply.github.com>
Date: Thu, 30 Apr 2026 15:09:05 -0700
Subject: [PATCH 04/12] fix(phoenix): include DownloadBarView height in
 installer auto-size

The bottom-anchored DownloadBarView is a ZStack overlay, so SettingsView's
ScrollView didn't know about it; when settings content fit, the last rows
sat hidden behind the bar (worse in the retry state, where the bar grows).

Add a second preference key (InstallerOverlayHeightKey) that reports the
bar's measured height to InstallerView. targetWindowHeight now adds it to
the connectors-screen target so the bar always sits below the content.
The key reverts to 0 when the bar leaves the view tree (post-install),
restoring the bar-less sizing.

Co-Authored-By: Claude Opus 4.7 (1M context) <noreply@anthropic.com>

From 328d02ed9dba2bad8e9a0ea673b2854deb5d95ee Mon Sep 17 00:00:00 2001
From: Swagat Patel <swagatpatel@users.noreply.github.com>
Date: Thu, 30 Apr 2026 18:13:16 -0700
Subject: [PATCH 09/12] test(phoenix): cover KeepMacAwake failed-acquire
 rollback

Add an injectable AssertionCreator closure (and a UserDefaults injection
point) on KeepMacAwake so a deterministic kIOReturnError can be fed in
without touching real IOKit.

KeepMacAwakeTests.testFailedAcquireRevertsToggleAndWritesOnceWithoutRecursion
asserts the round-2 contract: when acquire fails, isEnabled reverts to
false, UserDefaults persists false, the assertion creator is invoked
exactly once (no recursion through suppressApply), and exactly one
UserDefaults write of the resolved value occurs for the toggle.

The pre-fix didSet path would have left isEnabled=true and UserDefaults=
true after a failed acquire, so this test fails red against the pre-74908cac code.

Co-Authored-By: Claude Opus 4.7 (1M context) <noreply@anthropic.com>

diff --git a/app/PhoenixTests/KeepMacAwakeTests.swift b/app/PhoenixTests/KeepMacAwakeTests.swift
new file mode 100644
index 000000000..6399c57b0
--- /dev/null
+++ b/app/PhoenixTests/KeepMacAwakeTests.swift
@@ -0,0 +1,83 @@
+import IOKit.pwr_mgt
+import XCTest
+@testable import Phoenix
+
+@MainActor
+final class KeepMacAwakeTests: XCTestCase {
+    private var defaults: CountingUserDefaults!
+    private var suiteName: String!
+
+    override func setUp() {
+        super.setUp()
+        suiteName = "KeepMacAwakeTests-\(UUID().uuidString)"
+        defaults = CountingUserDefaults(suiteName: suiteName)!
+        defaults.removePersistentDomain(forName: suiteName)
+    }
+
+    override func tearDown() {
+        defaults.removePersistentDomain(forName: suiteName)
+        defaults = nil
+        suiteName = nil
+        super.tearDown()
+    }
+
+    // Toggle ON → IOPMAssertionCreateWithName fails → isEnabled must revert to
+    // false, UserDefaults must persist false, acquire must be tried exactly
+    // once (no recursion through suppressApply), and exactly one UserDefaults
+    // write of the final value must occur for the toggle interaction.
+    func testFailedAcquireRevertsToggleAndWritesOnceWithoutRecursion() {
+        defaults.set(false, forKey: KeepMacAwake.preferencesKey)
+
+        var acquireCallCount = 0
+        let failingAcquire: KeepMacAwake.AssertionCreator = {
+            acquireCallCount += 1
+            return (kIOReturnError, IOPMAssertionID(0))
+        }
+
+        let keepMacAwake = KeepMacAwake(userDefaults: defaults, assertionCreator: failingAcquire)
+        // After init: isEnabled=false, applyEnabled goes to else branch
+        // (releaseAssertion no-op) and writes false to UserDefaults. Reset
+        // the test counters AFTER init to scope the assertions to the
+        // toggle interaction.
+        XCTAssertFalse(keepMacAwake.isEnabled)
+        XCTAssertEqual(acquireCallCount, 0, "init should not attempt acquire when seeded false")
+        defaults.resetWriteCount(forKey: KeepMacAwake.preferencesKey)
+
+        // The pre-fix code would have left isEnabled=true and UserDefaults=true
+        // after this assignment because the failure path silently swallowed
+        // the IOReturn. The post-fix code reverts both, in a single bounded
+        // didSet round.
+        keepMacAwake.isEnabled = true
+
+        XCTAssertFalse(keepMacAwake.isEnabled, "isEnabled must revert to false when acquire fails")
+        XCTAssertFalse(
+            defaults.bool(forKey: KeepMacAwake.preferencesKey),
+            "UserDefaults must persist the reverted false, not the user's intended true"
+        )
+        XCTAssertEqual(
+            acquireCallCount, 1,
+            "acquireAssertion must be called exactly once — suppressApply gate must prevent the inner isEnabled=false write from re-entering applyEnabled and re-invoking the closure"
+        )
+        XCTAssertEqual(
+            defaults.writeCount(forKey: KeepMacAwake.preferencesKey), 1,
+            "exactly one UserDefaults write of the resolved value (false), not one per didSet pass"
+        )
+    }
+}
+
+/// Counts `set(_:forKey:)` invocations per key. Subclasses `UserDefaults` so
+/// it slots into `KeepMacAwake`'s injected dependency without a wrapper
+/// protocol; only the Bool overload is overridden because that's the one
+/// `KeepMacAwake.applyEnabled` calls.
+private final class CountingUserDefaults: UserDefaults {
+    private var counts: [String: Int] = [:]
+
+    override func set(_ value: Bool, forKey defaultName: String) {
+        counts[defaultName, default: 0] += 1
+        super.set(value, forKey: defaultName)
+    }
+
+    func writeCount(forKey key: String) -> Int { counts[key] ?? 0 }
+
+    func resetWriteCount(forKey key: String) { counts[key] = 0 }
+}

From d4462c22ee48785f2c9dcdb14733c8b34eef38f5 Mon Sep 17 00:00:00 2001
From: Swagat Patel <swagatpatel@users.noreply.github.com>
Date: Thu, 30 Apr 2026 18:33:26 -0700
Subject: [PATCH 10/12] fix(phoenix): renumber KeepMacAwakeTests pbxproj IDs to
 avoid main collision
MIME-Version: 1.0
Content-Type: text/plain; charset=UTF-8
Content-Transfer-Encoding: 8bit

The IDs FC00/FC01 I used for KeepMacAwakeTests collide with main's
PortAllocator.swift (introduced in #552, container feature). On a fresh
PR-merge build (CI's setup), the merged pbxproj has the same fileRef
ID assigned to two different paths, which Xcode 16.4 reports as:

  warning: file reference for "KeepMacAwakeTests.swift" is a member of
  multiple groups ("Phoenix" and "PhoenixTests"); this indicates a
  malformed project.

The Phoenix target's swift-frontend invocation then includes
KeepMacAwakeTests.swift in its source list, which fails with
"no such module 'XCTest'" because the main app target doesn't link
XCTest.

Local builds didn't trip this because my branch lacks main's container
files. Renumber to FF00/FF01 — verified unused in origin/main.

Co-Authored-By: Claude Opus 4.7 (1M context) <noreply@anthropic.com>

From 985517d998d09a2a5b7dc3a2fb22d5eba742fea7 Mon Sep 17 00:00:00 2001
From: Swagat Patel <swagatpatel@users.noreply.github.com>
Date: Fri, 1 May 2026 09:48:43 -0700
Subject: [PATCH 11/12] test(phoenix): cover KeepMacAwake init-path
 failed-acquire revert

The existing failed-acquire test seeds UserDefaults=false and only
exercises the toggle-ON path; init's explicit applyEnabled() call (which
runs when UserDefaults seeded true and acquire fails on launch) is not
covered. testFailedInitFromSeededTrueRevertsAndPersistsFalse seeds true,
injects a failing assertion creator, and asserts isEnabled reverts to
false, UserDefaults persists false, the creator is called exactly once,
and exactly one set(_:Bool, forKey:) write occurs during init.

If the init's applyEnabled() call were removed, the prior test would
still pass; this one catches that regression.

Co-Authored-By: Claude Opus 4.7 (1M context) <noreply@anthropic.com>
---
 app/PhoenixTests/KeepMacAwakeTests.swift | 34 ++++++++++++++++++++++++
 1 file changed, 34 insertions(+)

diff --git a/app/PhoenixTests/KeepMacAwakeTests.swift b/app/PhoenixTests/KeepMacAwakeTests.swift
index 6399c57b0..5168019ce 100644
--- a/app/PhoenixTests/KeepMacAwakeTests.swift
+++ b/app/PhoenixTests/KeepMacAwakeTests.swift
@@ -63,6 +63,40 @@ final class KeepMacAwakeTests: XCTestCase {
             "exactly one UserDefaults write of the resolved value (false), not one per didSet pass"
         )
     }
+
+    // Init-path coverage. The toggle test above never exercises init's
+    // explicit applyEnabled() call — if init's revert path were deleted, that
+    // test would still pass. This one seeds UserDefaults=true so init's
+    // applyEnabled is the *only* code path that runs.
+    func testFailedInitFromSeededTrueRevertsAndPersistsFalse() {
+        defaults.set(true, forKey: KeepMacAwake.preferencesKey)
+        defaults.resetWriteCount(forKey: KeepMacAwake.preferencesKey)
+
+        var acquireCallCount = 0
+        let failingAcquire: KeepMacAwake.AssertionCreator = {
+            acquireCallCount += 1
+            return (kIOReturnError, IOPMAssertionID(0))
+        }
+
+        let keepMacAwake = KeepMacAwake(userDefaults: defaults, assertionCreator: failingAcquire)
+
+        XCTAssertFalse(
+            keepMacAwake.isEnabled,
+            "init must revert isEnabled to false when the seeded-true UserDefaults causes acquire to be attempted and fail"
+        )
+        XCTAssertFalse(
+            defaults.bool(forKey: KeepMacAwake.preferencesKey),
+            "init must persist the reverted false to UserDefaults so the next launch reads false"
+        )
+        XCTAssertEqual(
+            acquireCallCount, 1,
+            "init's applyEnabled must call the assertion creator exactly once — not twice (recursion) and not zero (skipped init revert)"
+        )
+        XCTAssertEqual(
+            defaults.writeCount(forKey: KeepMacAwake.preferencesKey), 1,
+            "init's applyEnabled must write the final value exactly once"
+        )
+    }
 }

From 4d8d34c60b2aa47e81d9b129979fffdaf4cf4a91 Mon Sep 17 00:00:00 2001
From: Swagat Patel <swagatpatel@users.noreply.github.com>
Date: Fri, 1 May 2026 09:48:51 -0700
Subject: [PATCH 12/12] fix(phoenix): tighten Keep Mac Awake copy + add
 accuracy tooltip
MIME-Version: 1.0
Content-Type: text/plain; charset=UTF-8
Content-Transfer-Encoding: 8bit

The previous subtitle "Prevent system sleep so your agent stays
reachable" overpromised: kIOPMAssertionTypePreventSystemSleep doesn't
beat clamshell sleep, and on battery the kernel downgrades it to
PreventUserIdleSystemSleep — display sleep still happens.

Subtitle now reads "Prevent idle sleep so your agent stays reachable"
which matches the actual contract. A SwiftUI .help(...) tooltip on the
row spells out the constraints in layperson terms: lid open, plugged
in, battery downgrades to display-on idle-sleep prevention, and lid
close sleeps regardless.

Co-Authored-By: Claude Opus 4.7 (1M context) <noreply@anthropic.com>

   480	    private var installerSuccess: Color { PlowTheme.success }

   483	    private func animateWelcomeRows() {
   484	        showWelcomeRows = false
   485	        DispatchQueue.main.async {
   486	            showWelcomeRows = true
   487	        }
   488	    }
   489	
   490	    private func updateWindowHeight(for screen: InstallerScreen) {
   491	        guard let window = NSApp.windows.first(where: { $0.identifier?.rawValue == "installer" }) else { return }
   492	        let targetHeight: CGFloat = screen == .connectors ? 610 : 530
   493	        let currentFrame = window.frame
   494	        guard abs(currentFrame.height - targetHeight) > 1 else { return }
   495	        let newOriginY = currentFrame.origin.y + (currentFrame.height - targetHeight)
   496	        let newFrame = NSRect(
   497	            x: currentFrame.origin.x,
   498	            y: newOriginY,
   499	            width: currentFrame.width,
   500	            height: targetHeight
   501	        )
   502	        NSAnimationContext.runAnimationGroup { context in
   503	            context.duration = 0.45
   504	            context.timingFunction = CAMediaTimingFunction(name: .easeOut)
   505	            window.animator().setFrame(newFrame, display: true)
   506	        }
   507	    }
   508	}

     5	/// Holds `kIOPMAssertionTypePreventSystemSleep` while the user has opted in, so
     6	/// the system stays in Dark Wake and the OpenClaw VM keeps serving through
     7	/// display sleep. On battery the kernel effectively downgrades the assertion to
     8	/// idle-sleep-prevention, so a single assertion works in both power states;
     9	/// draining the battery is the user's explicit choice when they toggle this on.
    10	/// Clamshell sleep on a MacBook without an external display is still enforced
    11	/// by the kernel and isn't reachable from a public API.
    12	@Observable
    13	@MainActor
    14	final class KeepMacAwake {
    15	    static let preferencesKey = "keepMacAwakeWhileRunning"
    16	
    17	    /// Wraps `IOPMAssertionCreateWithName` so tests can inject deterministic
    18	    /// failure (kIOReturnError) without depending on real IOKit. The default
    19	    /// invokes the system call; tests pass a closure that returns whatever
    20	    /// (IOReturn, IOPMAssertionID) tuple they want.
    21	    typealias AssertionCreator = () -> (result: IOReturn, assertionID: IOPMAssertionID)
    22	
    23	    private let userDefaults: UserDefaults
    24	    private let assertionCreator: AssertionCreator
    25	
    26	    private var assertionID: IOPMAssertionID = 0
    27	    private var hasAssertion = false
    28	    /// Set true while we're rewriting `isEnabled` from inside `applyEnabled` to
    29	    /// revert a failed acquire — the recursive `didSet` reads this and bails.
    30	    private var suppressApply = false
    31	
    32	    var isEnabled: Bool {
    33	        didSet { applyEnabled() }
    34	    }
    35	
    36	    init(
    37	        userDefaults: UserDefaults = .standard,
    38	        assertionCreator: AssertionCreator? = nil
    39	    ) {
    40	        self.userDefaults = userDefaults
    41	        self.assertionCreator = assertionCreator ?? KeepMacAwake.defaultAssertionCreator
    42	        self.isEnabled = userDefaults.bool(forKey: KeepMacAwake.preferencesKey)
    43	        // didSet doesn't fire during init — explicitly run applyEnabled so the
    44	        // assertion is acquired (and the toggle reverted on failure) on launch
    45	        // when UserDefaults seeded `isEnabled = true`.
    46	        applyEnabled()
    47	    }
    48	
    49	    /// Releases the assertion deterministically before the app exits, so the
    50	    /// system can coalesce any pending sleep state at logout/shutdown without
    51	    /// waiting for process reaping. The kernel reclaims the assertion on
    52	    /// process exit either way; this is a courtesy, not a leak fix.
    53	    func teardown() {
    54	        releaseAssertion()
    55	    }
    56	
    57	    /// Brings the held IOPMAssertion in line with `isEnabled` and persists the
    58	    /// resolved value. If acquire fails, `isEnabled` is reverted to false so
    59	    /// the toggle UI matches the actual sleep-prevention state.
    60	    private func applyEnabled() {
    61	        guard !suppressApply else { return }
    62	        if isEnabled {
    63	            if !acquireAssertion() {
    64	                suppressApply = true
    65	                isEnabled = false
    66	                suppressApply = false
    67	            }
    68	        } else {
    69	            releaseAssertion()
    70	        }
    71	        userDefaults.set(isEnabled, forKey: Self.preferencesKey)
    72	    }
    73	
    74	    private func acquireAssertion() -> Bool {
    75	        guard !hasAssertion else { return true }
    76	        let outcome = assertionCreator()
    77	        guard outcome.result == kIOReturnSuccess else {
    78	            NSLog("[KeepMacAwake] IOPMAssertionCreateWithName failed: %d", outcome.result)
    79	            return false
    80	        }
    81	        assertionID = outcome.assertionID
    82	        hasAssertion = true
    83	        return true
    84	    }
    85	
    86	    private func releaseAssertion() {
    87	        guard hasAssertion else { return }
    88	        IOPMAssertionRelease(assertionID)
    89	        hasAssertion = false
    90	        assertionID = 0
    91	    }
    92	
    93	    private static let defaultAssertionCreator: AssertionCreator = {
    94	        var id: IOPMAssertionID = 0
    95	        let reason = "Plow keeping Mac awake so the OpenClaw VM stays reachable" as CFString
    96	        let result = IOPMAssertionCreateWithName(
    97	            kIOPMAssertionTypePreventSystemSleep as CFString,
    98	            IOPMAssertionLevel(kIOPMAssertionLevelOn),
    99	            reason,
   100	            &id
   101	        )
   102	        return (result, id)
   103	    }
   104	}

   108	    /// Fires when the user tries to "open" Plow while it's already running:

   110	    /// the moment to guarantee the UI is reachable — the menu bar button may
   111	    /// be hidden behind the notch, pushed off-screen by overflow, or tucked
   112	    /// away by Bartender, and the user just told us they want to see the app.
   113	    func applicationShouldHandleReopen(_ sender: NSApplication, hasVisibleWindows flag: Bool) -> Bool {

 succeeded in 0ms:
     1	import AppKit
     2	import Observation
     3	import SwiftUI
     4	
     5	/// Owns the app's menu-bar presence as a real NSStatusItem + NSPanel.
     6	///
     7	/// We used MenuBarExtra originally, but macOS silently hides the MenuBarExtra
     8	/// button when the menu bar overflows, when a notched display clips it, or when
     9	/// a third-party app (Bartender, Ice) collapses it into a tray. That left the
    10	/// *only* entry point to the app unreachable. NSStatusItem exposes the backing
    11	/// button and its window frame, which lets us detect the hide case and fall
    12	/// back to a programmatic panel anchor so the UI is always reachable.
    13	///
    14	/// The popover surface is an NSPanel rather than an NSPopover for two reasons:
    15	/// an NSPanel is kept around between shows (instant open — NSPopover tears down
    16	/// its hosting view each time), and NSPanel supports user resize so the agent
    17	/// activity log can be dragged to a larger height.
    18	@MainActor
    19	final class MenuBarController: NSObject, NSWindowDelegate {
    20	    private let statusItem: NSStatusItem
    21	    private let panel: MenuBarPanel
    22	    private let daemonClient: DaemonClient
    23	    private let isDev: Bool
    24	    private var globalClickMonitor: Any?
    25	    private var appearanceObservation: NSKeyValueObservation?
    26	
    27	    init(
    28	        daemonClient: DaemonClient,
    29	        downloadManager: RuntimeDownloadManager,
    30	        appUpdater: AppUpdater,
    31	        installerController: InstallerWindowController,
    32	        audioRecorder: AudioRecorder,
    33	        keepMacAwake: KeepMacAwake,
    34	        isDev: Bool
    35	    ) {
    36	        self.statusItem = NSStatusBar.system.statusItem(withLength: NSStatusItem.variableLength)
    37	        self.panel = MenuBarPanel(rootView:
    38	            StatusView(
    39	                client: daemonClient,
    40	                downloadManager: downloadManager,
    41	                appUpdater: appUpdater,
    42	                installerController: installerController,
    43	                audioRecorder: audioRecorder,
    44	                keepMacAwake: keepMacAwake
    45	            )
    46	        )
    47	        self.daemonClient = daemonClient
    48	        self.isDev = isDev
    49	        super.init()
    50	
    51	        panel.delegate = self
    52	
    53	        if let button = statusItem.button {
    54	            button.target = self
    55	            button.action = #selector(handleStatusItemClick(_:))
    56	            button.sendAction(on: [.leftMouseUp, .rightMouseUp])
    57	        }
    58	
    59	        startObservingHeaderState()
    60	        // KVO on the button's effectiveAppearance catches every menu-bar
    61	        // appearance change — system theme switches, wallpaper-driven
    62	        // transparency changes, and space transitions — unlike the
    63	        // AppleInterfaceThemeChangedNotification which only fires on
    64	        // light/dark mode toggles.
    65	        appearanceObservation = statusItem.button?.observe(
    66	            \.effectiveAppearance, options: [.new]
    67	        ) { [weak self] _, _ in
    68	            Task { @MainActor in self?.refreshIcon() }
    69	        }
    70	    }
    71	
    72	    deinit {
    73	        NSStatusBar.system.removeStatusItem(statusItem)
    74	    }
    75	
    76	    // MARK: - Icon
    77	
    78	    private func startObservingHeaderState() {
    79	        withObservationTracking {
    80	            _ = self.daemonClient.headerState
    81	        } onChange: { [weak self] in
    82	            Task { @MainActor in
    83	                guard let self else { return }
    84	                self.refreshIcon()
    85	                self.startObservingHeaderState()
    86	            }
    87	        }
    88	        refreshIcon()
    89	    }
    90
